Zertifikat Deutsch · telc Deutsch B1 · your complete guide

Everything you need to pass telc B1.

You are at A2.1 with two months to go. This guide is your single source: all the grammar explained simply, the vocabulary theme by theme with memory tricks, ready-made letters for the writing test, full dialogues for the speaking test, and an 8-week plan that tells you exactly what to do each day. Work through it and walk in prepared.

225
points, written exam · you need 135 (60%)
75
points, oral exam · you need 45 (60%)
2
parts graded separately — if you fail one, you retake only that one
~60
days until your exam · set date
How to use this guide: don't read it like a book. Follow the 8-week plan — it tells you which section to study each week. The Writing and Speaking sections are the biggest point-winners: they run on fixed phrases you can memorise even at A2 level.
Exam

The exam, part by part

READ THIS FIRST

Know every task, every point and every rule before you walk in. No surprises = calm brain = more points.

📝 The written exam — 225 points, 9 tasks

The written exam takes about 2½ hours of work plus a 20-minute break. It always runs in this order. All numbers below come from the official telc materials.

TaskWhat you getPointsStrategy in one line
Lesen 1
global		5 short texts + 10 headlines: match each text to its headline25
(5 × 5)		Read for the main idea, not every word.
Lesen 2
detail		1 long newspaper text + 5 multiple-choice questions (a/b/c)25
(5 × 5)		The questions follow the text order.
Lesen 3
selective		10 situations + 12 small ads: find the ad that fits each situation25
(10 × 2,5)		Check date, place, price. No fit? Mark x.
Sprachbausteine 1A letter with 10 gaps, 3 options per gap — grammar15
(10 × 1,5)		Connectors, prepositions, pronouns, verb forms.
Sprachbausteine 2A letter with 10 gaps, choose from 15 words — vocabulary15
(10 × 1,5)		Fixed word pairs. Fill the easy gaps first.
Hören 1
global		5 short texts, richtig/falsch — plays ONCE25
(5 × 5)		Read all 5 statements before the audio starts.
Hören 2
detail		1 long conversation/interview, 10 richtig/falsch — plays twice25
(10 × 2,5)		1st listen: answer. 2nd listen: check.
Hören 3
selective		5 announcements, richtig/falsch — plays twice25
(5 × 5)		Hunt for numbers, times, prices, places.
Brief
(writing)		1 semi-formal letter/e-mail answering 4 guiding points, 30 min45Cover all 4 points — that is most of the marks.
The clock, exactly: ① Reading + Sprachbausteine share one 90-minute block — you decide how to split it. ② 20-minute break. ③ Listening, about 30 minutes — the audio controls the speed, not you. ④ The letter is its own separate 30-minute block at the end.
Common mistake: people think they must "save" reading time for the letter. No! The letter has its own 30 minutes — you can never borrow time between blocks. So use the full 90 minutes for reading + gaps, and keep about 10 of those minutes to copy your answers onto the answer sheet (der Antwortbogen — the official sheet the machine reads; only answers on it count).

🗣️ The oral exam — 75 points, 3 parts, usually done in pairs

You normally speak with another candidate in front of two examiners (only if the number of candidates is odd is it done solo, with an examiner as your partner). The talk itself lasts at most 15 minutes per pair. Before it, you get 20 minutes of preparation time with the task sheets — you may write short notes, but in the exam you should talk, not read aloud.

PartWhat happensTime · Points
Teil 1
Kontaktaufnahme		"Making contact": you introduce yourselves and ask each other simple personal questions (job, family, hobbies, languages).3–4 min · 15
Teil 2
Gespräch über ein Thema		"Conversation about a topic": each of you gets short info on a topic; you report it, give your opinion and experience, and react to your partner.5–6 min · 30
Teil 3
Gemeinsam etwas planen		"Planning something together" (official name: Gemeinsam eine Aufgabe lösen — solving a task together): suggest, agree, disagree politely, decide together.5–6 min · 30

How the 75 points are scored

Both examiners score you separately during the talk, then agree on a mark after you leave the room. Each part is rated on the same four things:

  • Ausdrucksfähigkeit — expression: can you say what you mean, with enough words?
  • Aufgabenbewältigung — doing the task: did you really discuss / plan / react to your partner?
  • Formale Richtigkeit — accuracy: grammar and sentence building.
  • Aussprache und Intonation — pronunciation and melody: easy to understand?
Good news: at B1 level, examiners expect mistakes. A wrong article costs almost nothing. Silence, or ignoring your partner, costs a lot. Keep talking, keep reacting — that is what "Aufgabenbewältigung" rewards.

✅ Pass rules — super simple

  • Written exam: 225 points possible → you need 135 (that is 60%).
  • Oral exam: 75 points possible → you need 45 (also 60%).
  • You must reach 60% in both parts. A great written score cannot rescue a failed oral, and the other way round.
  • Failed (or missed) only ONE part? You may repeat just that part — in the same calendar year or the next one. The part you passed stays valid.
  • The whole exam can be repeated as often as you want.
Memory trickThink "60 + 60": sixty percent written and sixty percent oral. Two separate doors — you must open both.

Your grade (only if you passed both parts)

Add your written + oral points together (maximum 300):

GradeMeaningTotal points
sehr gutvery good270–300
gutgood240–269,5
befriedigendsatisfactory210–239,5
ausreichendsufficient (pass)180–209,5
For almost every purpose (job, visa, citizenship, university application) only "bestanden" (passed) matters, not the grade. Aim for solid 60–70% everywhere — don't chase perfection.

📖 Leseverstehen — how to beat each reading task

Teil 1 (headlines): read the text FIRST

Read one short text, say its main idea to yourself in your own words, and only then look for the headline that says the same idea. There are 10 headlines for 5 texts, so 5 headlines are traps.

The word trap: a wrong headline often repeats one word from the text. Same word ≠ same meaning. Match the idea, never a single word.

Teil 2 (long text): questions first, in order

Read the 5 questions before the text. The questions follow the text from top to bottom, so once you have found the answer to question 2, the answer to question 3 comes later in the text. Answer only from what the text says — not from your own knowledge or opinion.

Teil 3 (ads): every condition must fit

Underline the 2–3 conditions in each situation (what? when? how much?), then scan the 12 ads. An ad is only correct if all conditions fit. Each ad can be used only once — and if no ad fits a situation, you mark x on the answer sheet.

Situation: Sie suchen eine 2-Zimmer-Wohnung mit Balkon für maximal 600 Euro.Situation: You are looking for a 2-room flat with a balcony for a maximum of 600 euros.
Anzeige: Schöne 2-Zimmer-Wohnung mit Balkon, 650 Euro.Ad: Nice 2-room flat with balcony, 650 euros.
Balcony ✓, 2 rooms ✓ — but 650 > 600, so this ad is wrong. One broken condition kills the whole match. This is exactly how telc builds its traps.

Suggested time budget (inside the 90-minute block)

  • Lesen 1 — about 12 minutes
  • Lesen 2 — about 20 minutes
  • Lesen 3 — about 20 minutes
  • Sprachbausteine 1 + 2 — about 25 minutes
  • Copy answers to the Antwortbogen + final check — about 13 minutes

🧩 Sprachbausteine — 30 fast points if you know the patterns

Teil 1 tests grammar (10 gaps, 3 options each)

  • Connectors — weil vs. denn vs. deshalb vs. obwohl: the word order around the gap tells you the answer.
  • Prepositions — an, auf, für, mit, bei, von… mostly the fixed verb + preposition pairs.
  • Verb forms — würde/könnte/hätte, Perfekt forms, reflexive pronouns (mich, dich, sich).
  • Pronouns & articles — der/dem/den, relative pronouns, Ihnen vs. Sie.

Teil 2 tests vocabulary (10 gaps, choose from 15 words)

  • Fixed expressions and word partners: Vielen Dank für…, Ich freue mich auf…, typical letter phrases.
  • 15 words for 10 gaps → 5 words are never used. Fill the easy gaps first; every filled gap removes a word and makes the hard gaps easier.
  • Check what word type the gap needs: after "der/die/das" a noun, before a noun maybe an adjective, in position 2 a verb.
Method for every gap: read the whole sentence (often also the sentence before), decide what type of word is missing, then test each option quietly "by ear". Never answer a gap in isolation.
There is no penalty for wrong answers — so never, ever leave a gap empty. A blind guess is free points waiting to happen.
✍️ Try it — a mini Sprachbausteine round 4 gaps

Fill the gaps in this short reply to a course office:

Liebe Frau Berg,

vielen Dank (1) ___ Ihre E-Mail. Ich kann leider nicht am Kurs teilnehmen, (2) ___ ich nächste Woche arbeiten muss. Ich würde mich freuen, (3) ___ Sie mir einen anderen Termin anbieten könnten. Ich warte (4) ___ Ihre Antwort.

Viele Grüße
Sadman

  • (1) a) für   b) über   c) um
  • (2) a) denn   b) deshalb   c) weil
  • (3) a) dass   b) wenn   c) ob
  • (4) a) an   b) auf   c) für

Answers — and why

GapAnswerWhy
1a) fürFixed pair: danken für — you always thank FOR something.
2c) weilThe verb "muss" sits at the end of the clause → that is "weil" word order. With "denn" the verb would come earlier: "…, denn ich muss arbeiten."
3b) wenn"I would be happy IF you could…" — "dass" (= that) and "ob" (= whether) don't fit the meaning here.
4b) aufFixed pair: warten auf + Akkusativ (the case for the thing the action points at).
Vielen Dank für Ihre E-Mail.Thank you very much for your e-mail.
Ich kann leider nicht am Kurs teilnehmen, weil ich nächste Woche arbeiten muss.Unfortunately I can't take part in the course, because I have to work next week.
Ich würde mich freuen, wenn Sie mir einen anderen Termin anbieten könnten.I would be happy if you could offer me another date.
Ich warte auf Ihre Antwort.I am waiting for your reply.

🎧 Hörverstehen — listening without panic

The golden rule: read the statements BEFORE the audio

The recording always gives you time to read the statements first — use every second of it. Underline the key word in each statement. While listening, you only check: does the audio say this, yes or no?

Teil 1 plays only ONCE. This is the hardest moment of the whole exam — there is no second chance. Train this part more than any other: full focus for those few minutes, phone away, one listen only when you practise.

Teil 2 (long conversation, plays twice)

The 10 statements follow the conversation in order. First listen: answer everything you can. Second listen: check and fix. If you missed one, mark a guess and let it go — richtig/falsch means even a blind guess wins half the time.

Teil 3 (announcements, plays twice): hunt the numbers

Announcements at stations, on answering machines, on the radio. The answer almost always hides in a number, time, price, date or platform — write these down the moment you hear them.

Der Zug nach Hamburg fährt heute um 14:30 Uhr von Gleis 7.The train to Hamburg leaves today at 2:30 pm from platform 7.
Never chase a lost answer. If you miss item 47, guess it and jump to item 48. Chasing one answer into the next text is how people lose three points instead of one.

🎒 Exam day — your checklist

Pack the bag the evening before. Tap each item to tick it off:

  • Personalausweis or passport — the SAME ID you used when you registered
  • Registration confirmation / invitation letter from the exam centre
  • Check the address, room and travel route one day before
  • Arrive 30 minutes early — late arrivals can be refused
  • Two pens (blue or black) + a pencil for notes
  • Water bottle and a small snack for the 20-minute break
  • Phone switched OFF and in your bag — smartwatches count as phones
  • No dictionary, no notes, no vocabulary lists on the desk
  • Glasses or hearing aid if you need them
  • On the way there: listen to easy German audio to warm up your ears
  • The night before: sleep well, review only old notes — nothing new
  • During the exam: watch the clock and never leave any answer empty
Last thought before the door: you do not need 100%. You need 60% — and you are allowed to make mistakes in every single part and still pass. Breathe, read the task, collect the easy points first. Viel Erfolg! (Good luck!)
Plan

Your 8-week plan (A2.1 to B1)

START HERE

Today is 14 July 2026. Your exam is in mid-September. That is about 8–9 weeks. With 1.5–2.5 focused hours a day, you can do this.

How to study with this guide

This guide is your whole course. The plan below tells you when to open which section. The method is simple and it works:

  • Grammar first (weeks 1–2). Grammar is the skeleton. Without Perfekt (the past tense for talking) and word order, you cannot build sentences — so we fix that first.
  • Vocabulary every single day — no exceptions. Use spaced repetition (a fancy name for a simple idea): every day you learn 10 new words, then you review yesterday’s words, then you review the words from the same day last week. Repeating at growing distances is what moves words into long-term memory.
  • Writing from week 3. The letter in the exam follows a fixed recipe. You will write at least one letter every week from week 3 on, so that on exam day it is routine, not stress.
  • Speaking out loud from week 4. Your mouth needs training, not only your eyes. Talk to yourself, to your mirror, to your phone’s voice recorder. Nobody is listening — but the muscles learn.
  • Mock timing from week 7. In the last two weeks you practise with a real clock. Knowing the answer is not enough; you must find it in the given time.

The “chunk” principle — the most important study rule

Never learn a word alone. Always learn it inside a short sentence (a “chunk”). A single word gives you nothing to say; a chunk gives you a ready-made piece of German. It also stores the article, the case and the preposition for free.

Not just “der Termin” — learn: Ich habe einen Termin beim Arzt.I have an appointment at the doctor’s.
Not just “warten” — learn: Ich warte auf den Bus.I am waiting for the bus.
Memory trickOne chunk = word + article + case + preposition, all in one package. Ten chunks a day beat fifty naked words a day.
You do not need perfect German for B1. You need about 60% of the points. Steady daily work beats talent — every single time.

The 8 weeks, week by week

Each week: which sections of this guide to study, what to memorise, and one goal you can measure. The daily 20-min vocab routine (10 new + review yesterday + review last week) runs through all eight weeks.

Week 1 (14–20 Jul) — Know the enemy, fix the basics
  • Study: the exam format section (what is tested, how many points), then core grammar: articles, cases, present tense, word order (Grammar).
  • Memorise: 70 words in chunks from the everyday themes (Vocabulary): family, daily life, food.
  • Goal: say 20 correct sentences about yourself and your day, out loud, without notes.
  • Daily 20 min: 10 new chunks + review yesterday’s.
Week 2 (21–27 Jul) — The past tense engine
  • Study: Perfekt with hat / ist, Präteritum of haben, sein and the modal verbs (Grammar).
  • Memorise: the irregular verb list — the participles (ge-forms) of the 60 most common verbs. This list pays for itself in every exam part.
  • Goal: write 10 sentences about yesterday in Perfekt with zero auxiliary mistakes (hat or ist?).
  • Daily 20 min: vocab routine + say each new chunk once in the past tense.
Week 3 (28 Jul–3 Aug) — Connect ideas + first letters
  • Study: subordinate clauses with weil, dass, wenn and the connectors deshalb, trotzdem (Grammar); then the letter recipe (Writing).
  • Memorise: the fixed letter phrases (greeting, opening, closing) by heart — word for word.
  • Goal: write 1 complete informal letter in 40 minutes, covering all the given points.
  • Daily 20 min: vocab routine + one weil-sentence out loud about your day.
Week 4 (4–10 Aug) — Prepositions + start speaking
  • Study: Dativ, two-way prepositions, adjective endings (Grammar); the self-introduction for the oral exam (Speaking).
  • Memorise: your personal 1-minute self-introduction until it runs automatically.
  • Goal: record yourself: 2 minutes about yourself + write 1 formal letter in 35 minutes.
  • Daily 20 min: vocab routine, spoken out loud from now on — every chunk gets said, not only read.
Week 5 (11–17 Aug) — Reading + Sprachbausteine
  • Study: reading strategies for all reading parts (Reading) and the grammar-in-context part (Sprachbausteine).
  • Memorise: 70 more chunks: work, health, travel themes (Vocabulary).
  • Goal: finish one full reading set in 45 minutes + write 1 letter in 30 minutes.
  • Daily 20 min: vocab routine + underline every connector you meet while reading.
Week 6 (18–24 Aug) — Listening + speaking parts 2 and 3
  • Study: listening strategies (Listening) and the oral exam parts “talk about a topic” and “plan something together” (Speaking).
  • Memorise: the discussion phrases (agreeing, disagreeing, suggesting) — they carry the whole oral exam.
  • Goal: do the listening part of the official telc Übungstest once and count your points honestly.
  • Daily 20 min: vocab routine + 20 extra minutes of German audio (see the listening card below).
Week 7 (25–31 Aug) — Mock exams with the clock
  • Study: nothing new. Do the full official telc Übungstest under real time: Lesen + Sprachbausteine 90 min, Hören 30 min, Schreiben 30 min. Then check answers and re-study your weak sections in this guide.
  • Memorise: re-review ALL old vocab days (the spaced-repetition harvest week).
  • Goal: write 2 letters in 30 minutes each — complete, with greeting and closing.
  • Daily 20 min: vocab review only (no new words this week).
Week 8 (1 Sep–exam) — Repair, simulate, taper
  • Study: second full mock at the start of the week. Fix the two weakest parts. Simulate the oral exam daily: introduction + topic + planning, out loud, with a timer.
  • Memorise: only your personal “mistake list” — the words and forms YOU got wrong in the mocks.
  • Goal: reach 60% or more in the second mock.
  • Daily 20 min: review your mistake list + one letter opening/closing from memory.
Last 2 days before the exam: NO new material. Only read your cheat sheet (letter phrases, discussion phrases, irregular verbs) and sleep well. A tired brain loses more points than a small grammar gap.

Daily routine template (about 80–100 minutes)

Same shape every day. Routine removes decisions, and fewer decisions means you actually start.

  • 20 min — Vocabulary: 10 new chunks out loud + review yesterday + review the same weekday last week.
  • 30 min — Section work: the grammar or skill section your current week says (see the plan above). Read a little, then DO a lot: write examples, cover the answers, test yourself.
  • 20 min — Listening: one item from the listening table below. Listen once without text, once with.
  • 10 min — Speaking out loud: tell yourself what you did today, in German, in the past tense. Yes, alone. Yes, out loud.
  • +20 min — Writing (from week 3): a letter or half a letter, with a timer.
Heute habe ich Deutsch gelernt und zwanzig Minuten laut gesprochen.Today I studied German and spoke out loud for twenty minutes.
Busy day? Do the 20-min vocab block anyway. A short day keeps the chain alive; a zero day breaks it.

Listening practice — be honest with yourself

Honest warning: this guide is a text file. It cannot play audio. But Hörverstehen (listening) is worth 75 points — a third of the written exam. If you skip real audio training, this guide cannot save you. All sources below are free:

WhatWhyHow often
Official telc Deutsch B1 Übungstest (free PDF + MP3 from telc.net)The single most important dry run: real exam tasks, real audio, real timing, with answer key.Once in week 6 (listening only), full test in weeks 7 and 8.
DW “Nicos Weg” B1 (free video course, learngerman.dw.com)A full free B1 course as a story — clear speech, subtitles, exercises after each video.3–4 short episodes per week, weeks 1–6.
DW “Langsam gesprochene Nachrichten” (slowly spoken news, with transcript)Real news read slowly; the transcript lets you check every word you missed.Daily from week 3, 10 minutes.
Easy German (YouTube)Real street interviews with German + English subtitles — trains you for natural, fast speech.2–3 videos per week.
DW “Top-Thema mit Vokabeln”Short B1-level audio texts with a vocabulary list — listening and vocab in one.2 per week from week 4.
Nachrichtenleicht (nachrichtenleicht.de)The week’s news in simple German, audio + text — gentle start when normal news feels too fast.Once a week, e.g. Saturday.
Do NOT only read this guide. Reading feels like progress, but the exam tests writing, listening and speaking too. Rule of thumb: for every hour of reading, spend one hour writing, listening or speaking out loud.
Print or download the official Übungstest early (week 1), so it is ready when week 6 comes. Seeing the real exam paper early also kills a lot of fear.
Grammar 1

Grammar I – nouns, cases & adjectives

THE FOUNDATION

Cases, articles, pronouns, prepositions and adjective endings – the skeleton of every German sentence.

1. The four cases – what are they?

German nouns change their article depending on their job in the sentence. There are four jobs, called "cases". Don't panic: at B1 you really only need three of them well.

CaseJob in the sentenceAsk
N Nominativthe subject – who does the actionWer? Was?
A Akkusativthe direct object – the thing the action happens toWen? Was?
D Dativthe receiver – to/for whom; also after Dativ prepositionsWem?
G Genitivpossession ("of the …") – rare at B1, mostly after wegen, während, trotzWessen?
Der Mann liest eine Zeitung.The man is reading a newspaper. (der Mann = subject = Nominativ)
Ich sehe den Mann.I see the man. (the man "receives" the seeing = Akkusativ)
Ich helfe dem Mann.I help the man. (helfen always takes Dativ)
Das ist das Auto des Mannes.That is the man's car. (Genitiv – rare, you mostly just need to recognise it)

Definite article: der / die / das ("the")

NAD
der (m)der Mannden Manndem Mann
die (f)die Fraudie Frauder Frau
das (n)das Kinddas Kinddem Kind
die (Plural)die Kinderdie Kinderden Kindern
Genitiv (rare): des Mannes, der Frau, des Kindes, der Kinder. Masculine and neuter nouns also get -s / -es at the end: des Mannes, des Kindes.
Memory trickIn Akkusativ, only masculine changes: der → den. Everything else sleeps! And in Dativ plural the noun itself gets an extra -n: mit den Kindern, mit den Freunden (only -s plurals refuse: mit den Autos).

Demonstrative article: dieser / diese / dieses ("this")

dieser points at something specific ("this one here"). Great news: it takes exactly the same endings as der/die/das – just swap d- for dies-:

NAD
der (m)dieser Manndiesen Manndiesem Mann
die (f)diese Fraudiese Fraudieser Frau
das (n)dieses Kinddieses Kinddiesem Kind
Pluraldiese Kinderdiese Kinderdiesen Kindern
Dieser Pullover gefällt mir.I like this pullover. (Nominativ, masculine → dieser)
Ich nehme diesen Kuchen.I'll take this cake. (Akkusativ, masculine → diesen)
"That one over there" is usually just der/die/das … da in everyday German: Ich möchte das Brot da. (I'd like that bread there.) The old word jener ("that") sounds very formal – you only need to recognise it.
Memory trickder → dieser, die → diese, das → dieses, den → diesen, dem → diesem. The der/die/das table IS the dieser table – you already know it!

Indefinite article: ein ("a / an") and kein ("no / not a")

NAD
der (m)ein / keineinen / keineneinem / keinem
die (f)eine / keineeine / keineeiner / keiner
das (n)ein / keinein / keineinem / keinem
Plural— / keine— / keine— / keinen
ein has no plural (like English: "a books" is impossible). kein does: Ich habe keine Kinder. Genitiv: eines Mannes, einer Frau, eines Kindes.

Possessive words: my, your, his, her …

These take exactly the same endings as kein. You only need to pick the right base word:

PersonBase wordEnglish
ichmeinmy
dudeinyour (informal)
er / esseinhis / its
sieihrher
wirunserour
ihreueryour (you all)
sieihrtheir
SieIhryour (formal, capital I!)
Das ist meine Schwester und das ist ihr Mann.This is my sister and this is her husband.
Ich fahre mit meinem Bruder zu unseren Eltern.I'm driving with my brother to our parents. (Dativ endings)
euer loses its middle e when it gets an ending: euer Haus, but eure Kinder, mit eurem Auto.

2. Personal pronouns – I, me, to me

Pronouns change with the case, just like articles. Learn this table by heart – it appears in every exam part.

NADEnglish
ichmichmirI / me / to me
dudichdiryou (informal)
erihnihmhe / him
siesieihrshe / her
esesihmit
wirunsunswe / us
ihreucheuchyou all
siesieihnenthey / them
SieSieIhnenyou (formal)
Memory trickmir, dir, ihr all rhyme – if it rhymes with "hier", it's Dativ. And uns / euch are lazy: they look the same in Akkusativ and Dativ.
Kannst du mir helfen? Ich rufe dich später an.Can you help me? I'll call you later.
Wie geht es Ihnen? – Danke, gut!How are you (formal)? – Fine, thanks!

2b. Indefinite pronouns – man, jemand, nichts …

These little words replace people or things you don't name exactly. They appear constantly in Sprachbausteine and listening texts – learn them as a set.

WordEnglishExample
manone / you / people in generalIn Deutschland trennt man den Müll.
jemandsomebodyJemand hat an der Tür geklingelt.
niemandnobodyNiemand war zu Hause.
etwassomethingMöchtest du etwas trinken?
nichtsnothingIch habe nichts gehört.
alleeverybody / allAlle sind gekommen.
vielemany (people)Viele fahren mit dem Rad zur Arbeit.
einigesome / a fewEinige waren mit dem Kurs nicht zufrieden.
In Deutschland trennt man den Müll.In Germany, people separate their rubbish. (In general – nobody specific.)
Kennst du hier jemanden? – Nein, ich kenne niemanden.Do you know anybody here? – No, I know nobody. (Akkusativ: jemanden / niemanden)
Ich habe etwas für dich – und es kostet nichts!I have something for you – and it costs nothing!
Viele lernen Deutsch, aber nur wenige sprechen jeden Tag.Many (people) learn German, but only few speak every day.
NAD
manmaneineneinem
jemandjemandjemandenjemandem
niemandniemandniemandenniemandem
etwas / nichtsetwas / nichtsetwas / nichtsetwas / nichts
Wenn man krank ist, hilft einem der Arzt.When one is sick, the doctor helps one. (Dativ of man = einem)
man always takes the er/sie/es verb form: man trennt, man kann, man muss. Don't confuse it with der Mann (the man) – one n! etwas and nichts never change their form, in any case. alle, viele, einige, wenige are plural and take plural verb forms: Alle sind da. Viele kommen.
Memory trickPositive/negative pairs: jemand ↔ niemand (somebody ↔ nobody), etwas ↔ nichts (something ↔ nothing). People bend (jemanden, jemandem), things don't (etwas and nichts are frozen).

3. Verbs that force Dativ

Some verbs always put their object in Dativ, even though English uses a normal object. The exam loves these!

VerbEnglishMini example
helfento helpIch helfe dir.
dankento thankIch danke Ihnen.
gefallento please (I like it)Das gefällt mir.
gehörento belong toDas gehört mir.
schmeckento taste (good)Es schmeckt mir.
antwortento answer (a person)Antworte mir!
glaubento believe (a person)Ich glaube dir.
folgento followFolgen Sie mir!
gratulierento congratulateIch gratuliere dir!
passento fitDie Hose passt mir.
wehtunto hurtMir tut der Kopf weh.
fehlento be missing / missedDu fehlst mir.
Ich helfe meiner Mutter am Wochenende im Garten.I help my mother in the garden on the weekend.
Das Handy gehört meinem Bruder, nicht mir.The phone belongs to my brother, not to me.
Die neue Wohnung gefällt uns sehr gut.We like the new flat very much. (literally: it pleases us)

Verbs with two objects: person = D, thing = A

Verbs like geben, schenken, zeigen, bringen, erklären, empfehlen (give, gift, show, bring, explain, recommend) have a person AND a thing. The person gets Dativ, the thing gets Akkusativ.

Ich gebe meinem Freund das Buch.I give my friend the book. (friend = Dativ, book = Akkusativ)
Er erklärt mir die Aufgabe.He explains the task to me.
Können Sie uns ein gutes Restaurant empfehlen?Can you recommend a good restaurant to us?
Memory trickThink of a gift: the person receives (Dativ = receiver), the thing is given away (Akkusativ). Person first, thing second: Ich schenke dir eine Blume.

4. Prepositions – which case do they want?

Prepositions are small words like "with, for, in". Each one is married to a case. Learn them in groups – there is a song rhythm for each group.

🎯 Always Akkusativ 7 preps
PrepEnglishExample
durchthroughWir gehen durch den Park.
fürforDas Geschenk ist für dich.
gegenagainstDas Auto fährt gegen den Baum.
ohnewithoutEr kommt ohne seine Frau.
umaround / at (time)Wir sitzen um den Tisch.
bisuntilIch bleibe bis nächsten Sonntag.
entlangalongWir gehen die Straße entlang.
entlang is special: it comes after the noun – die Straße entlang.
Memory trickSay it as a rap: durch – für – gegen – ohne – um. Five beats, all Akkusativ.
🎯 Always Dativ 9 preps
PrepEnglishExample
ausfrom / out ofIch komme aus der Türkei.
beiat (a person/place)Er wohnt bei seinen Eltern.
mitwithIch fahre mit dem Bus.
nachafter / to (cities)Nach dem Essen gehen wir spazieren.
seitsince / for (time)Ich lerne seit einem Jahr Deutsch.
vonfrom / ofDas ist ein Geschenk von meiner Oma.
zuto (people/places)Ich gehe zum Arzt.
abfrom (time on)Ab nächster Woche habe ich Urlaub.
gegenüberoppositeDie Bank ist gegenüber dem Bahnhof.
Memory trickSing aus – bei – mit – nach – seit – von – zu to the melody of the "Blue Danube" waltz. Millions of German learners do this – it works!
🎯 Genitiv preps (rare but tested) 4 preps
PrepEnglishExample
währendduringWährend des Sommers arbeite ich.
wegenbecause ofWegen des Wetters bleiben wir zu Hause.
trotzdespiteTrotz des Regens gehen wir raus.
stattinstead ofStatt eines Autos kaufe ich ein Fahrrad.
In spoken German, people often use Dativ here: "wegen dem Wetter". For the written exam, Genitiv looks better – but nobody fails because of this.

Wechselpräpositionen – the "two-way" prepositions

Nine prepositions can take both cases: an, auf, hinter, in, neben, über, unter, vor, zwischen (at/on, on top of, behind, in, next to, above, under, in front of, between). The rule:

QuestionMeaningCase
Wo?Where is it? (position, no movement)D Dativ
Wohin?Where to? (movement to a new place)A Akkusativ
Die Katze springt auf den Tisch.The cat jumps onto the table. (movement → Akkusativ)
Die Katze sitzt auf dem Tisch.The cat sits on the table. (position → Dativ)
Ich hänge das Bild an die Wand. Jetzt hängt es an der Wand.I hang the picture on the wall. Now it hangs on the wall.
Memory trickAkkusativ = Action (something moves to a new place). Dativ = Dort ("there" – it just stays there). Ask yourself: is anything travelling? Yes → Akkusativ. No → Dativ.

Contractions – short forms everyone uses

  • im = in dem (im Bett – in bed)
  • am = an dem (am Montag – on Monday)
  • ins = in das (ins Kino – to the cinema)
  • zum = zu dem (zum Arzt – to the doctor)
  • zur = zu der (zur Arbeit – to work)
  • beim = bei dem (beim Frühstück – at breakfast)
  • vom = von dem (vom Bahnhof – from the station)

5. Adjective endings – made survivable

When an adjective stands before a noun, it needs an ending: der alte Mann. There are three patterns, depending on what comes before the adjective. Don't try to be perfect – learn the patterns roughly plus the shortcut below.

Pattern 1: after der / die / das

der alte Mann – ich sehe den alten Mannthe old man – I see the old man
NAD
der-e-en-en
die-e-e-en
das-e-e-en
Plural-en-en-en

Pattern 2: after ein / kein / mein …

ein alter Mann – eine alte Frau – ein altes Autoan old man – an old woman – an old car
NAD
ein-er-en-en
eine-e-e-en
ein-es-es-en
Plural (keine)-en-en-en

Pattern 3: no article at all

Ich trinke gern kaltes Wasser mit frischer Zitrone.I like drinking cold water with fresh lemon.
NAD
m-er-en-em
f-e-e-er
n-es-es-em
Plural-e-e-en
Memory trickThe lifesaver shortcut: after der/die/das, whenever it is Dativ, Genitiv or plural, the ending is -en. When in doubt in the exam, -en is right far more often than anything else.

The 5 endings the exam tests again and again

Ich suche eine neue Wohnung.I'm looking for a new flat. (Akkusativ, feminine → -e)
Wir haben einen sehr netten Lehrer.We have a very nice teacher. (Akkusativ, masculine → -en)
Ich wohne in einer kleinen Stadt.I live in a small town. (Dativ → -en)
Das ist ein interessantes Buch.That is an interesting book. (Nominativ, neuter → -es)
Mit freundlichen GrüßenWith kind regards. (Dativ plural, no article → -en; you write this in every exam letter!)

6. Comparative & superlative – bigger, biggest

Comparative (comparing two things): adjective + -er. Superlative (the top one): am + adjective + -sten. Short adjectives with a, o, u often get an umlaut (ä, ö, ü).

BasicComparativeSuperlative
alt (old)älteram ältesten
groß (big)größeram größten
jung (young)jüngeram jüngsten
gut (good)besseram besten
viel (much)mehram meisten
gern (gladly)lieberam liebsten
hoch (high)höheram höchsten
nah (near)näheram nächsten
Mein Bruder ist älter als ich.My brother is older than me.
Anna ist genauso alt wie ich.Anna is just as old as me.
Ich trinke lieber Tee als Kaffee, aber am liebsten trinke ich Wasser.I prefer tea to coffee, but most of all I like drinking water.
Die Tage werden immer kürzer.The days are getting shorter and shorter. (immer + comparative = "more and more")
Memory trickals = different (bigger THAN), wie = same (as big AS). Think: als = anders (different), wie sounds like "we" – we are equal!

7. n-Deklination – the sticky -n nouns

A small club of masculine nouns adds -n / -en in every case except Nominativ. They are almost all male people (or animals).

N NominativA/D/GEnglish
der Jungeden/dem Jungenboy
der Kollegeden/dem Kollegencolleague
der Kundeden/dem Kundencustomer
der Menschden/dem Menschenhuman, person
der Herrden/dem Herrngentleman, Mr
der Nachbarden/dem Nachbarnneighbour
der Studentden/dem Studentenstudent
der Praktikantden/dem Praktikantenintern
Ich frage den Kollegen.I'll ask the colleague.
Kennen Sie Herrn Müller? Ich schreibe Herrn Müller eine E-Mail.Do you know Mr Müller? I'm writing an email to Mr Müller. (very important for exam letters!)

8. Negation – kein or nicht?

Two ways to say "not". The decision rule is simple:

UseWhenExample
kein-before a noun that has ein or no articleIch habe kein Auto.
nichteverything else: verbs, adjectives, names, der/die/das-nounsIch komme nicht.
Ich trinke keinen Kaffee. (ein Kaffee → kein Kaffee)I don't drink coffee.
Er kommt heute nicht.He is not coming today. (nicht at the end negates the whole sentence)
Die Wohnung ist nicht groß.The flat is not big. (nicht directly before the adjective)
Wir fahren nicht nach Spanien, sondern nach Italien.We are not going to Spain, but to Italy. (nicht directly before the place phrase)
Memory trickPosition of nicht: normally at the end of the sentence – but it jumps directly in front of the thing it denies (an adjective, a place, "sondern …" contrasts).

9. Question words – W-Fragen

❓ All question words with examples 16 words
WordEnglishExample
wer NwhoWer ist das?
wen AwhomWen besuchst du?
wem Dto whomWem gehört die Tasche?
wessen GwhoseWessen Auto ist das?
waswhatWas machst du?
wowhere (position)Wo wohnst du?
wohinwhere toWohin fährst du?
woherwhere fromWoher kommst du?

warumwhyWarum lernst du Deutsch?

welch- takes the same endings as der/die/das: welcher Mann, welche Frau, welches Kind, welchen Film (Akkusativ m).
Wem gehört dieser Schlüssel? – Er gehört meinem Nachbarn.Whose key is this (to whom does it belong)? – It belongs to my neighbour.
Wohin fahrt ihr im Urlaub? – Woher weißt du, dass wir Urlaub haben?Where are you going on holiday? – How (from where) do you know we have a holiday?

10. Noun genders & plurals – rules of thumb

Yes, you must learn genders. But some noun endings give the gender away almost every time:

🔎 Gender rules of thumb 12 rules
EndingGenderExample
-ungdie alwaysdie Wohnung (flat)
-heit / -keitdie alwaysdie Gesundheit (health), die Möglichkeit (possibility)
-schaftdie alwaysdie Freundschaft (friendship)
-iondie alwaysdie Information (information)
-tätdie alwaysdie Universität (university)

-e / ¨-emany der/das nounsder Tisch → die Tische; die Stadt → die Städte

Memory trickNever learn "Wohnung = flat". Always learn article + noun + plural as ONE word: "dieWohnung-dieWohnungen". Say it out loud like one long word. Your future self in the exam will thank you.
Good news: in the plural, all genders become die. And examiners at B1 forgive small gender mistakes in speaking – they never forgive missing communication. Keep talking!
Grammar 2

Grammar II — the verb system

LEARN THIS COLD

Verbs carry the whole German sentence. If words like "tense" or "past form" confuse you, read block 0 first — it explains the whole system with zero grammar knowledge needed.

0. START HERE — the time machine 🕐 (what is a "tense"?)

A verb is a doing-word: trinken (to drink), machen (to make), gehen (to go). A "tense" is nothing scary — it just means the doing-word changes its clothes to show WHEN it happens. There are only three times in life:

⬅️ GESTERN (yesterday)🔴 JETZT (now)➡️ MORGEN (tomorrow)
Ich habe Kaffee getrunken.Ich trinke Kaffee.Morgen trinke ich Kaffee.
I drank coffee.I am drinking coffee.Tomorrow I'll drink coffee.
The best news in this whole guide: look at that table again. NOW and TOMORROW use the same word (trinke)! German needs only TWO verb forms for 95% of everything you will ever say: the now-form (Präsens) and the yesterday-form (Perfekt). That's it. Two forms. You can do this.

Rule 1 — NOW and TOMORROW: the now-form does both jobs

For anything happening now, use the now-form. For the future, use the same form + a time word (morgen, nächste Woche, am Samstag). No new grammar needed!

Ich lerne Deutsch.I am learning German. (now)
Morgen lerne ich Deutsch.Tomorrow I will learn German. (future = same verb + time word!)
Am Samstag besuche ich meine Freunde.On Saturday I will visit my friends.

Rule 2 — YESTERDAY: the sandwich trick 🥪

To say something already happened, German builds a sandwich. Two pieces of bread hold the sentence together, and everything else sits in the middle:

Who🍞 Helper (place 2)The middle🍞 ge-word (very end)
Ichhabegestern Kaffeegetrunken.
Wirhabeneine Pizzagemacht.
Ichbingestern nach Bonngefahren.

Baby recipe, three steps every single time:

  • Step 1: say who + the helper: Ich habe… (or Ich bin…)
  • Step 2: say everything else: when, what, where.
  • Step 3: throw the ge-word to the very END: …gemacht / gegessen / gefahren.
Ich habe am Wochenende meine Wohnung geputzt.I cleaned my flat at the weekend.
Er hat gestern einen Film gesehen.He watched a film yesterday.
Sie ist heute früh aufgestanden.She got up early today.
Which helper — habe or bin?Ask one baby question: "Did the person walk / travel / change?" 🚶 Moving or changing → bin/ist (Ich bin gefahren, er ist aufgestanden). Everything else → habe/hat (Ich habe gegessen, sie hat gekauft). Details in block 5.

Rule 3 — the three little cheats: war, hatte, wollte

For "was", "had" and "wanted/could/had to", Germans skip the sandwich and use one short word instead. Learn these three sentences and copy them forever:

Ich war gestern krank.I was ill yesterday. (NOT a sandwich — just "war")
Ich hatte keine Zeit.I had no time.
Ich musste arbeiten.I had to work. (also: wollte = wanted, konnte = could)

The whole system on one plate 🍽️

You want to say…UseExample
happening nownow-formIch lerne Deutsch.
tomorrow / laternow-form + time wordMorgen lerne ich Deutsch.
yesterday (most verbs)sandwich: habe/bin + ge-wordIch habe Deutsch gelernt.
was / hadwar / hatteIch war müde. Ich hatte Glück.
wanted / could / had towollte / konnte / mussteIch konnte nicht kommen.
promise or guess about laterwerden + verb (block 8)Es wird morgen regnen.
If you remember only one thingJETZT = verb in place 2 and finished. GESTERN = helper (habe/bin) in place 2 + ge-word at the very end. Say one "now" sentence and one "yesterday" sentence about your day, out loud, every morning — after two weeks the sandwich builds itself.
Blocks 1, 5, 6, 7 and 8 below teach each piece properly (endings, the ge-word patterns, war/hatte tables). Read block 0 again whenever you feel lost — it is the map.

1. Present tense (Präsens)

The present tense does two jobs: now (Ich lerne Deutsch) and future with a time word (Morgen lerne ich Deutsch).

How to build it: every verb in the dictionary ends in -en (machen, trinken, lernen). Cut off the -en — what is left is called the stem (mach-, trink-, lern-). Then glue the right ending onto the stem for each person:

Regular endings

PersonEndingmachen
ich-emache
du-stmachst
er / sie / es-tmacht
wir-enmachen
ihr-tmacht
sie / Sie-enmachen
Stems ending in -t / -d add an extra e: du arbeitest, er arbeitet, er findet. Stems ending in -s / -ß / -z lose the s in du: du heißt, du tanzt (not "heißst").

Stem-changing verbs (only in du and er/sie/es!)

Some strong verbs change their vowel — but ONLY in the du and er/sie/es forms. Wir, ihr, sie stay normal.

ChangeVerbser/sie/es form
e → iessen, geben, helfen, nehmen, sprechen, treffenisst, gibt, hilft, nimmt, spricht, trifft
e → ielesen, sehen, empfehlenliest, sieht, empfiehlt
a → äfahren, schlafen, tragen, waschen, einladenfährt, schläft, trägt, wäscht, lädt ein
Er nimmt den Bus und fährt zur Arbeit.He takes the bus and drives to work.
Sie liest gern und spricht drei Sprachen.She likes reading and speaks three languages.
Common mistake: "du nehmst", "er fahrt". Wrong! It is du nimmst, er fährt. The change only touches du + er/sie/es: wir fahren, ihr fahrt are regular.

The 4 super-verbs — know them cold

⭐ Full tables: sein, haben, werden, wissen 24 forms
Personsein (to be)haben (to have)
ichbinhabe
dubisthast
er/sie/esisthat
wirsindhaben
ihrseidhabt
sie/Siesindhaben
Personwerden (to become)wissen (to know a fact)
ichwerdeweiß
duwirstweißt
er/sie/eswirdweiß
wirwerdenwissen
ihrwerdetwisst
sie/Siewerdenwissen
Ich weiß nicht, wo er ist.I don't know where he is.
Das Wetter wird morgen besser.The weather is getting better tomorrow.

2. Separable and inseparable verbs

Many German verbs are built from a prefix + a verb: an + rufen = anrufen (to call). Some prefixes jump to the end of the sentence (separable), some never move (inseparable).

Separable prefixes (stressed: ANrufen) — with meaning hints

PrefixFeeling / hintExamples
ab-off, awayabfahren (depart), abholen (pick up)
an-on, at, startanrufen (call), anfangen (begin)
auf-up, openaufstehen (get up), aufmachen (open)
aus-out, offausgehen (go out), ausmachen (turn off)
ein-in, intoeinkaufen (shop), einsteigen (get in)
mit-along, withmitkommen (come along), mitbringen (bring along)
nach-after, againnachdenken (think about), nachfragen (ask again)
vor-before, forwardvorbereiten (prepare), vorstellen (introduce)
zu-closed, towardszumachen (close), zuhören (listen)
zurück-backzurückkommen (come back), zurückrufen (call back)
weg-awayweggehen (go away), wegfahren (drive away)

Inseparable prefixes (never move, never stressed)

THE 7 GLUED PREFIXES
  • be- — besuchen (to visit)
  • emp- — empfehlen (to recommend)
  • ent- — entschuldigen (to excuse)
  • er- — erklären (to explain)
  • ge- — gefallen (to please)
  • ver- — verstehen (to understand)
  • zer- — zerbrechen (to break into pieces)
Memory trickChant it like a spell: be-emp-ent-er-ge-ver-zer. These 7 are glued on forever — they never separate, and in Perfekt they take no ge- (besucht, verstanden).

Where does the prefix go? The 4 positions

Main clause: Ich rufe dich morgen an.I'll call you tomorrow. (prefix flies to the end)
With a modal: Ich muss dich morgen anrufen.I have to call you tomorrow. (verb whole again, at the end)
Perfekt: Ich habe dich gestern an·ge·rufen.I called you yesterday. (-ge- sits IN THE MIDDLE: angerufen)
weil-clause: Ich habe keine Zeit, weil ich meine Mutter anrufe.I have no time because I'm calling my mother. (verb whole, at the end)
Never write "Ich anrufe dich." In a normal main clause the prefix MUST go to the end: Ich rufe dich an.

3. Modal verbs — the mood setters

Modal verbs add a flavour (can, must, may…) to another verb. The modal sits in position 2; the other verb goes to the end as an infinitive.

Meanings

ModalMeaningExample feel
könnencan, be able toability, possibility
müssenmust, have tonecessity
dürfenmay, be allowed topermission
sollenshould, be supposed tosomeone else wants it
wollenwant tostrong wish, plan
möchtenwould like topolite wish
mögento likelikes + noun: Ich mag Tee.
📋 Modal conjugation — present + Präteritum 2 tables

Golden pattern in the present: ich = er/sie/es (no ending!), and wir / sie / Sie = the infinitive.

Verbich / erduihr
könnenkannkannstkönnt
müssenmussmusstmüsst
dürfendarfdarfstdürft
sollensollsollstsollt
wollenwillwillstwollt
möchtenmöchtemöchtestmöchtet
mögenmagmagstmögt

Präteritum (past — modals almost always use this form, NOT Perfekt). Endings: ich -e, du -est, er -e, wir -en, ihr -et, sie -en… actually simpler: ich konnte, du konntest, er konnte, wir konnten, ihr konntet, sie konnten.

Verbich / er (past)English
könnenkonntecould, was able to
müssenmusstehad to
dürfendurftewas allowed to
sollensolltewas supposed to
wollenwolltewanted to
mögen / möchtenmochte / wollteliked / wanted
Note: the Umlaut disappears in the past — können → konnte, müssen → musste. And "möchten" has no own past: use wollte.
Als Kind durfte ich nicht lange fernsehen.As a child I wasn't allowed to watch TV for long.
Ich konnte gestern nicht kommen, weil ich arbeiten musste.I couldn't come yesterday because I had to work.

The Satzklammer — the sentence bracket

Modal in position 2, everything else in the middle, infinitive at the very end. Like two hands holding the sentence:

Position 1Pos. 2 = modalMiddleEND = infinitive
Ichmussheute noch Deutschlernen.
Morgenkannich leider nichtkommen.
Ermöchteam Wochenende Fußballspielen.
THE trap: "nicht müssen" ≠ "must not"!
Du musst nicht kommen. = You don't HAVE to come (it's optional).
Du darfst nicht kommen. = You must NOT come (forbidden!).
English "must not" = German nicht dürfen. Test writers love this one.

4. Imperative — giving instructions

Three forms, one for each way of saying "you":

WhoRuleExample
dustem, no -st, no "du"Komm! Nimm! Fahr langsam!
ihrnormal ihr-form, no "ihr"Kommt! Nehmt! Fahrt langsam!
Sieinfinitive + SieKommen Sie! Fahren Sie langsam!
Nimm deine Jacke mit — es ist kalt!Take your jacket with you — it's cold! (e→i verbs keep the change: nehmen → Nimm!)
Sei bitte ruhig! / Seid ruhig! / Seien Sie ruhig!Please be quiet! ("sein" is irregular: Sei / Seid / Seien Sie)
a→ä verbs do NOT change in the imperative: Fahr! (not "Fähr!"), Schlaf gut! But e→i/ie verbs DO: Nimm! Gib! Lies! Sprich!
The Sie-form is a free gift: infinitive + Sie. Kommen Sie herein! Nehmen Sie Platz! You'll use it constantly in the speaking test.

5. Perfekt — THE spoken past tense

When Germans talk about the past, they use Perfekt: haben/sein in position 2 + Partizip II at the end. In the letter and speaking parts, Perfekt is your default past tense.

Ich habe gestern eine Pizza gegessen.I ate a pizza yesterday.
Wir sind am Samstag nach Köln gefahren.We went to Cologne on Saturday.

haben or sein?

Use ist (sein) only for:

  • Movement from A to B: gehen, fahren, kommen, fliegen, laufen, steigen, umziehen → ist gegangen, ist gefahren
  • Change of state: aufstehen, einschlafen, aufwachen, sterben → ist aufgestanden, ist eingeschlafen
  • The special four: sein, bleiben, passieren, werden → ist gewesen, ist geblieben, ist passiert, ist geworden

Everything else takes hat: hat gemacht, hat gekauft, hat gesehen.

Memory trickAsk: "Did somebody or something move or change?" Yes → sein. No → haben. Plus learn "ist geblieben" as a weird exception — no movement, but still sein!

Building the Partizip II — 5 patterns

TypePatternExamples
regularge…tgemacht, gekauft, gearbeitet
irregularge…engegangen, geschrieben, genommen
-ieren verbs…iert (NO ge-)studiert, telefoniert, repariert
inseparableno ge-besucht, verstanden, erklärt
separable…ge… in the middleangerufen, eingekauft, aufgestanden
Ich habe zwei Jahre in Berlin studiert und dort viele Leute kennengelernt.I studied in Berlin for two years and met many people there.
Don't write "gestudiert" or "gebesucht"! -ieren verbs and be-/ver-/er-… verbs never get ge-: studiert, besucht, verstanden.

6. Präteritum — the written past

Newspapers and stories use Präteritum. For the exam you must actively use only these: war, hatte, the modals, es gab. Everything else you just need to recognise while reading.

war & hatte — full tables (use these instead of "ist gewesen / hat gehabt")

Personsein → warhaben → hatte
ichwarhatte
duwarsthattest
er/sie/eswarhatte
wirwarenhatten
ihrwarthattet
sie/Siewarenhatten
Ich war letzte Woche krank und hatte hohes Fieber.I was ill last week and had a high fever.
Früher gab es hier einen kleinen Markt.There used to be a small market here. ("es gab" = past of "es gibt")
Germans say ich war / ich hatte / ich musste even when speaking. Using them makes you sound natural AND saves you from building long Perfekt forms.
👀 Reading radar: 24 Präteritum forms you must recognise 24 forms

You will meet these in the reading texts. You don't have to produce them — just know what they mean.
